   <div class="abstract-group  metis-abstract">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" id="abstract-graphical-en">
         <h2 id="d97637209" class="article-section__header section__title graphical abstractlang_en graphical">Graphical Abstract</h2>
         <div class="graphical-abstract">
            <p>A systematic comparative study on the electrochemical performance and dynamic microstructural and mechanical changes of antimony anode in storing Li<sup>+</sup>/Na<sup>+</sup> cations was carried out by combining in situ microscopic and spectroscopic analysis, density functional theory simulations, and finite-element analysis. Full stepwise crystal transition with large lattice strains has been witnessed in Li-storage system, while an unexplored two-stage alloying/de-alloying mechanism with polycrystalline and amorphous phases as intermediates is discovered in Na-storage system, featuring enhanced resilience and less strain, contributing to high capacities and superior cyclability.
            </p><figure>
               <div class="center graphic__image"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/0b50705e-ae74-4829-85f4-e68530ab0f11/anie202320183-toc-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/0b50705e-ae74-4829-85f4-e68530ab0f11/anie202320183-toc-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/05f5a1ee-d150-4fd9-ba1f-feb57ec8f940/anie202320183-toc-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/0b50705e-ae74-4829-85f4-e68530ab0f11/anie202320183-toc-0001-m.jpg" alt="Description unavailable" title="Description unavailable" loading="lazy"></picture></a><p class="caption-style"></p>
               </div>
            </figure>
            <p></p>
            </div>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d97637212" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Alloying-type antimony (Sb) with high theoretical capacity is a promising anode candidate for both lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) and sodium-ion batteries (SIBs). Given the larger radius of Na<sup>+</sup> (1.02 Å) than Li<sup>+</sup> (0.76 Å), it was generally believed that the Sb anode would experience even worse capacity degradation in SIBs due to more substantial volumetric variations during cycling when compared to LIBs. However, the Sb anode in SIBs unexpectedly exhibited both better electrochemical and structural stability than in LIBs, and the mechanistic reasons that underlie this performance discrepancy remain undiscovered. Here, using substantial in situ transmission electron microscopy, X-ray diffraction, and Raman techniques complemented by theoretical simulations, we explicitly reveal that compared to the lithiation/delithiation process, sodiation/desodiation process of Sb anode displays a previously unexplored two-stage alloying/dealloying mechanism with polycrystalline and amorphous phases as the intermediates featuring improved resilience to mechanical damage, contributing to superior cycling stability in SIBs. Additionally, the better mechanical properties and weaker atomic interaction of Na−Sb alloys than Li−Sb alloys favor enabling mitigated mechanical stress, accounting for enhanced structural stability as unveiled by theoretical simulations. Our finding delineates the mechanistic origins of enhanced cycling stability of Sb anode in SIBs with potential implications for other large-volume-change electrode materials.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202320183-sec-0001">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202320183-sec-0001-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Despite the successful commercialization of lithium-ion batteries (LIBs), the sustainability and high-cost challenges of lithium sources make LIBs a more expensive choice for serving as power sources in large-scale energy storage.<span><sup><a href="#anie202320183-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202320183-bib-0001_R_d97637201e773" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1">1</a></sup></span> Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) with low cost, natural abundance, and evenly geographical distribution of sodium resources have thus been regarded as affordable alternatives for grid-scale stationary energy storage.<span><sup><a href="#anie202320183-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202320183-bib-0004_R_d97637201e776" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2">2</a></sup></span> However, SIBs are facing challenges in searching for appropriate electrode candidates that can stably host larger Na<sup>+</sup> ions with high performance, particularly anode materials with high capacity and stable cycling performance.<span><sup><a href="#anie202320183-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202320183-bib-0009_R_d97637201e781" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3">3</a></sup></span> Although SIBs share similar working principles with the widely studied LIBs, it has come to light that the technically viable anode materials for SIBs are largely distinct from those for LIBs.<span><sup><a href="#anie202320183-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202320183-bib-0014_R_d97637201e784" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Nano Lett.">4</a></sup></span> The electrochemical performance discrepancy is generally believed to be related to their different ionic sizes of Li<sup>+</sup> (0.76 Å) and Na<sup>+</sup> (1.02 Å) and molar volumes of Na- and Li- compounds, indicating that anode materials reacting with larger Na<sup>+</sup> ions normally experience more substantial volumetric variations and cause more severe structural deterioration during cycling than with Li<sup>+</sup> ions, which are most likely to detrimentally affect their cycling stability for SIBs.<span><sup><a href="#anie202320183-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202320183-bib-0015_R_d97637201e796" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5">5</a></sup></span> The observed dissimilarities, however, are not expected to be trivially related to the different ionic sizes.</p>
         
         <p>It is important to stress that the nanoscale reaction mechanisms of a given anode material in LIBs and SIBs including nanoscale reaction pathways, localized structural and mechanical changes of phases along with the morphological evolutions during cycling may also play a critical role in determining its electrochemical performance.<span><sup><a href="#anie202320183-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202320183-bib-0018_R_d97637201e802" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6">6</a></sup></span> For instance, despite larger volume changes during the conversion reaction of FeS<sub>2</sub> anode with Na<sup>+</sup>, it was reported to be only fracture during reaction with Li<sup>+</sup>, which is related to different nanoscale reaction pathways observed during lithiation/sodiation process contributing to the divergent fracture behavior.<span><sup><a href="#anie202320183-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202320183-bib-0022_R_d97637201e811" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Joule">7</a></sup></span> Previous research has also demonstrated similar anomalously facile Na<sup>+</sup> storage in ZnO,<span><sup><a href="#anie202320183-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202320183-bib-0023_R_d97637201e817" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Small Methods">8</a></sup></span> Cu<sub>2</sub>S,<span><sup><a href="#anie202320183-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202320183-bib-0024_R_d97637201e822" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - J. Mater. Chem. A">9</a></sup></span> and WS<sub>2</sub><span><sup><a href="#anie202320183-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202320183-bib-0025_R_d97637201e826" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Small">10</a></sup></span> anodes. Despite these intriguing findings, the effects of anode materials upon electrochemical reactions with Li<sup>+</sup> and Na<sup>+</sup> that induce their dynamic structural, morphological, and mechanical changes at the nanometer scale on electrochemical performance remain largely unexplored.<span><sup><a href="#anie202320183-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202320183-bib-0026_R_d97637201e834" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Mater.">11</a></sup></span> A better mechanistic understanding of the underlying Li<sup>+</sup>/Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanism would benefit the optimization and design of more efficient anode materials to further enhance the overall battery performance.<span><sup><a href="#anie202320183-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202320183-bib-0027_R_d97637201e841" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Chem. Mater.">12</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>In situ characterization techniques, especially in situ transmission electron microscopy (TEM), have high spatial and temporal resolutions to quantitatively monitor the dynamic nanoscale reaction processes of anode materials that were not achievable with other techniques, which provide a deep understanding of the fundamentals of the anode performance under battery operating conditions.<span><sup><a href="#anie202320183-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202320183-bib-0028_R_d97637201e846" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13">13</a></sup></span> Furthermore, by correlating the in situ TEM results with computational methodology, a more comprehensive elucidation of underlying Li<sup>+</sup>/Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanism in anode materials can be realized. Specifically, theoretical calculations and chemomechanical modeling of stress evolution are useful tools to discover the possible reaction paths and stress tolerated during cycling.<span><sup><a href="#anie202320183-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202320183-bib-0031_R_d97637201e853" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14">14</a></sup></span> Amongst a wealth of anode candidates, metallic antimony (Sb) storing Li<sup>+</sup> and Na<sup>+</sup> ions through alloying reactions with high theoretical capacity and suitable working potential, has been regarded as one of the most promising anode materials for both LIBs and SIBs.<span><sup><a href="#anie202320183-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202320183-bib-0036_R_d97637201e861" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15">15</a></sup></span> Until now, the volumetric expansion of this anode during lithiation or sodiation has mostly been regarded as responsible for structural instability and capacity fading during cycling.<span><sup><a href="#anie202320183-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202320183-bib-0041_R_d97637201e864" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Nat. Nanotechnol.">16</a></sup></span> Given the larger ionic radius of Na<sup>+</sup> ions, inferior cycling stability of the Sb anode caused by larger volumetric variations associated with more extensive mechanical degradation is generally expected during the insertion/extraction of Na<sup>+</sup> ions as compared to Li<sup>+</sup> ions.<span><sup><a href="#anie202320183-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202320183-bib-0042_R_d97637201e874" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">17</a></sup></span> Surprisingly, it is unexpected that the Sb anode in SIBs demonstrates superior structural stability and stable cyclic performance than that of its in LIBs, although larger volume expansion is observed during reaction with Na<sup>+</sup> ions when compared with Li<sup>+</sup> ions. And, the effects of ionic carriers on dynamic microstructural and mechanical variations of Sb anode during reaction, which are essential for battery performance and stability, are yet to be understood.</p>
         
         <p>In this study, we seek to uncover the origin of the surprising difference in dynamic reaction mechanisms of Sb anode during Li<sup>+</sup> and Na<sup>+</sup> ions insertion/extraction at the microscopic and macroscopic levels to clarify the counter-intuitive phenomenon of cycling performance. Utilizing substantial in situ experimental methods including in situ TEM coupled with geometric phase analysis (GPA), in situ X-ray diffraction (XRD), and in situ Raman techniques complemented by density functional theory (DFT) calculations and chemomechanical modeling of stress evolution using finite element analysis (FEA), we reveal that Sb reacting with Li<sup>+</sup> go through a stepwise crystal transition alloying/dealloying process suffering from large lattice strain accumulation which consequently responsible for poor cycling stability, whereas the reaction process in the Na/Sb system does not simply resemble its mechanism in the Li/Sb system and displays a previously unexplored two-stage alloying/dealloying mechanism with polycrystalline and amorphous phases as the intermediates featuring improved resilience to mechanical damage, contributing to superior cycling performance. Moreover, as jointly unveiled by DFT and FEA simulations, the as-formed Na−Sb alloys show better mechanical properties and weaker atomic interaction than Li−Sb alloys, enabling mitigated mechanical stress and accounting for enhanced structural stability. The combined experimental and modeling studies demonstrate that more severe volumetric variations during reaction do not necessarily induce accelerated capacity degradation, and the unforeseen nanoscale reaction pathways associated with related phase transformations also play an indispensable role in determining the stability of a battery</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202320183-sec-0002">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202320183-sec-0002-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>The Sb nanoflakes are synthesized by a molten-salt-assisted method (details described in the Supporting Information).<span><sup><a href="#anie202320183-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202320183-bib-0043_R_d97637201e899" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">18</a></sup></span> The XRD pattern of Sb nanoflakes in Figure <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0001">1a</a> shows sharp diffraction peaks that match well with the standard pattern of the hexagonal Sb phase with a space group of <i>R-3m</i> (PDF #35-0732), indicating its high crystallinity and purity. The corresponding atomistic structure model is given in Figure <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0001">1b</a> where the Sb phase consists of infinite chains of zigzag sheets along the <i>c</i>-axis.<span><sup><a href="#anie202320183-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202320183-bib-0044_R_d97637201e913" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Mater. Chem. Front.">19</a></sup></span> The surface chemical composition and electronic states of the Sb nanoflakes were verified using X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) measurements. The signals of Sb are visible in the full spectra (Figure <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0001">1c</a>). The binding energy of the Sb 3d spectrum in Figure <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0001">1d</a> is verified by the two strong peaks at 537.34 and 527.98 eV, corresponding to Sb 3d<sub>3/2</sub> and 3d<sub>5/2</sub>, respectively.<span><sup><a href="#anie202320183-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202320183-bib-0045_R_d97637201e926" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Energy Environ. Sci.">20</a></sup></span> Representative scanning electron microscope (SEM) and TEM (Figure <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0001">1e</a>, <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0001">f</a>) images of Sb nanoflakes exhibit a uniform and interconnected nanoflake morphology. Scanning transmission electron microscopy (STEM) image and energy dispersive spectroscopy (EDS) mapping in Figure <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0001">1g</a>, <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0001">h</a> not only demonstrate the uniform distribution of the Sb element but also indicate the high purity. Figure <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0001">1i</a> shows a single crystal structure based on the select area electron diffraction (SAED) patterns along the [010] zone axis, which is consistent with the Sb phase with a space group of <i>R-3m</i>. The acquired atomic-resolution high-angle angular dark field-STEM (HAADF-STEM) image and corresponding line profile in Figure <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0001">1j</a>–<a href="#anie202320183-fig-0001">l</a> are consistent with the atomistic structure along the [100].
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202320183-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/42dc1d94-30ac-4e8c-91dc-3b1786ed52de/anie202320183-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/42dc1d94-30ac-4e8c-91dc-3b1786ed52de/anie202320183-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/135bd79b-6221-4caf-b7bd-7d9d0f9389f0/anie202320183-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/42dc1d94-30ac-4e8c-91dc-3b1786ed52de/anie202320183-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202320183-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202320183" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Structural characterization of Sb nanoflakes. (a) XRD pattern. (b) Side and top view of the orthorhombic Sb crystal. (c, d) XPS spectrums. (e) SEM image. (f) Low-magnification TEM image. (g) Low-magnification STEM image. (h) EDS mapping. (i) SAED patterns. (j) Atomic-resolution HAADF-STEM image. (k, l) Line profile of the region highlighted by the dashed white box in (j).</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The Li and Na storage performances of Sb nanoflakes were evaluated in the half-cell configuration. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0002">2a</a>, the Sb nanoflakes as anode for LIBs delivered a discharge capacity of 894.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and a charge capacity of 786.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> in the first cycle, corresponding to an initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) of 87.9 %. While it exhibited a discharge capacity of 718.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and a charge capacity of 609.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with an ICE of 84.8 % when applied as the anode for SIBs (Figure <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0002">2b</a>). The initial capacity loss in both LIBs and SIBs can be mainly ascribed to the formation of solid electrolyte interface (SEI) and irreversible reactions.<span><sup><a href="#anie202320183-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202320183-bib-0046_R_d97637201e994" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Nat. Energy">21</a></sup></span> However, even with a higher initial capacity in LIBs as compared to SIBs, the capacity of Sb nanoflakes anode in LIBs deteriorated rapidly during cycling, retaining a low reversible capacity of 296.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with only 49.7 % capacity retention after 50 cycles at 0.05 A g<sup>−1</sup>, as depicted in Figure <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0002">2c</a>. In contrast, the Sb nanoflakes in SIBs maintained a high reversible capacity of 557.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 50 cycles with a high capacity retention of 86.7 % at the same test condition. Furthermore, the rate performance of Sb nanoflakes for both LIBs and SIBs was evaluated, as shown in Figure <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0002">2d</a>. Similarly, Sb nanoflakes in LIBs delivered higher capacities compared with their SIBs counterpart. Specifically, the Sb nanoflakes showed Li storage capacities of 734.5, 735.5, 707.9, 673.3, and 648.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at the increasing current densities of 0.05, 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, and 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, which are higher than those of their Na storage capacities at the same current densities (610.7, 582.3, 554.4, 510.7, and 440.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.05, 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, and 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>), as compared in Figure S1. Interestingly, when the current density recovered to 0.05 A g<sup>−1</sup> for another 40 cycles, the Sb nanoflakes anode in SIBs exhibited much better cycling stability (78.8 % capacity retention) as compared to LIBs (only 26.3 % capacity retention). Figure <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0002">2e</a> presents the cycling performance of the Sb nanoflakes anode in LIBs and SIBs at a higher current density of 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup>. The capacity in LIBs fades even faster after 20 cycles as compared to SIBs, only 13.7 % of the initial capacity was retained. This phenomenon indicates that at higher current density, the structure of Sb nanoflakes deteriorates much faster, which may originate from the stress accumulation during the lithiation/delithiation process. Impressively, the Sb nanoflakes anode in SIBs exhibited better cycling stability, and 59.1 % capacity retention was retained after 80 cycles. Figure S2 presents the morphology and microstructure change of electrodes after 80 cycles at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup> in both LIBs and SIBs. As shown in Figure S2a, obvious cracks can be found in the cycled Sb nanoflakes anode in LIBs. At the individual Sb nanoflake level, TEM images in Figure S2b-d further clearly show that the cycled Sb nanoflake became fragmented into a highly fluffy morphology, suggesting poor structural integrity. The signal of F, P, C, and O in Figure S2e originating from SEI can be detected in the elemental mapping of cycled Sb nanoflakes. The cycled electrode in SIBs remained well without drastic morphological changes (Figure S2f). TEM observation in Figure S2g further demonstrates the preservation of the nanoflake structure and the high-resolution TEM (HRTEM, Figure S2h) with its corresponding Fast Fourier Transform (FFT) image (Figure S2i) shows the crystalline nature, demonstrating the robust structural stability of the Sb nanoflakes anode in SIBs. Elemental mapping in Figure S2j proves the structural stability of the Sb nanoflake in SIB as well. All the above evidence indicates that Sb nanoflakes working as anode in SIBs exhibit better structural stability as compared to LIBs.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202320183-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/9eb30c5b-c72c-443c-9824-7fa05da84a0d/anie202320183-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/9eb30c5b-c72c-443c-9824-7fa05da84a0d/anie202320183-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/355e4f04-ee44-4e55-8d4c-00c1cf98957f/anie202320183-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/9eb30c5b-c72c-443c-9824-7fa05da84a0d/anie202320183-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202320183-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202320183" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Electrochemical tests for Sb nanoflakes in Li and Na half cells. (a, b) Galvanic discharge/charge profiles, (c) cycling performance, (d) rate performance, (e) long-term cycling performance for Sb nanoflakes in Li and Na half cells. (f, h) CV curves at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. (g, i) Ratios of capacitive contribution at different scan rates. (j, k) Diffusion coefficients during lithiation/delithiation and sodiation/desodiation process.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Furthermore, cyclic voltammetry (CV) tests were applied to deeply investigate the Li/Na storage mechanism of the Sb nanoflakes anode (Figure <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0002">2f</a>, <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0002">h</a>). During the first cycle, a sharp cathodic peak located at ~0.64 V (in LIBs) or ~0.35 V (in SIBs) was found, which is ascribed to the formation of the SEI. Whereas, in subsequent cycles, the peak is divided into two peaks (in LIBs) or three peaks (in SIBs), corresponding to the lithiation and sodiation process of Sb nanoflakes, respectively. As for the anodic sweep, peaks at ~1.2 V and ~0.9 V are found in the initial cycle for LIBs and SIBs, respectively.<span><sup><a href="#anie202320183-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202320183-bib-0047_R_d97637201e1063" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22">22</a></sup></span> During the second cycle, two peaks are displayed during the desodiation process, while for the delithiation process, only one peak at ~1.2 V was observed. This result suggests that the sodiation/desodiation process for the Sb nanoflakes anode is much more complex than that in the Li-system. Besides, to further understand the kinetics mechanism of the Sb nanoflakes anode, CV tests at various scan rates were conducted (Figure S3). The curves at different scan rates exhibit similar shapes but with slight peak shifts and narrow gaps which is mainly ascribed to the decreased electrode polarization. Moreover, according to the information obtained from CV curves at different scan rates, the capacitive and diffusive contributions can be determined using the equation <i>i</i>=<i>k<sub>1</sub>v</i>+<i>k<sub>2</sub>v</i><sup>1/2</sup>, where <i>i</i> represents the peak current, <i>v</i> is the scan rate, <i>k<sub>1</sub></i> and <i>k<sub>2</sub></i> stand for the capacitive and diffusion processes, respectively.<span><sup><a href="#anie202320183-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202320183-bib-0051_R_d97637201e1093" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">23</a></sup></span> The capacitive contribution ratio for both LIBs and SIBs is increasing with the increased scan rate (Figure <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0002">2g</a>, <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0002">i</a>). Apparently, the capacitive contribution in LIBs is larger than that in SIBs. The capacitive contribution for LIBs and SIBs reaches 96 % and 48 %, respectively. Thus, the Li<sup>+</sup> storage behavior is capacitive-controlled, but the diffusion-controlled behavior mainly dominates the electrochemical reaction in SIBs.<span><sup><a href="#anie202320183-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202320183-bib-0052_R_d97637201e1104" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Nat. Mater.">24</a></sup></span> The capacitive-controlled electrochemical reaction leads to fast reaction kinetics compared with the diffusion-controlled reaction, which is consistent with the rate tests as shown in Figure <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0002">2d</a>. Moreover, the diffusion coefficient of the Sb nanoflakes anode was evaluated by galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT), as shown in Figure S4. The diffusion coefficient of Sb nanoflakes anode was quantified by Fick's second law, and the results are displayed in Figure <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0002">2j</a>, <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0002">k</a>.<span><sup><a href="#anie202320183-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202320183-bib-0053_R_d97637201e1117" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - J. Power Sources">25</a></sup></span> Consequently, the diffusion coefficient of Sb nanoflakes in LIBs is higher than the value in SIBs at most potential, suggesting a faster diffusion of Li<sup>+</sup> ions, coinciding with the rate performance.</p>
         
         <p>To reveal the reasons for the superior electrochemical performance of Sb nanoflakes anode in SIBs over LIBs, in situ high-resolution TEM was applied to capture the lithiation/sodiation processes of the Sb nanoflakes inside the TEM.<span><sup><a href="#anie202320183-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202320183-bib-0054_R_d97637201e1125" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Adv. Energy Mater.">26</a></sup></span> Figure S5 shows the schematic experimental setup of the Li/Na ion battery in a half-cell configuration inside a TEM, in which the Sb nanoflakes anode is dispersed on a half TEM grid (Cu TEM grid with thin carbon film) serving as the working electrode, and the Li/Na metal on a W rod as the counter electrode. A layer of naturally formed Li<sub>2</sub>O/Na<sub>2</sub>O on the surface of the Li/Na source during the sample transfer process serves as the solid electrolyte. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0003">3a</a>–<a href="#anie202320183-fig-0003">c</a> (captured from Movie S1), HRTEM images are obtained at different stages of lithiation with the corresponding FFT patterns. Before reaction, pristine Sb nanoflake exhibits a hexagonal structure, and the lattice fringe of 2.1 Å is well indexed to the Sb [110] plane. At the first reaction step, Li<sup>+</sup> ions react with the Sb nanoflake, forming a hexagonal-structured Li<sub>2</sub>Sb alloy (P<i>62c</i>, No. 190). Both the HRTEM image and the FFT pattern indicate the formation of the Li<sub>2</sub>Sb phase because the lattice fringe and diffraction spots match well with the [101], [1<img alt="urn:x-wiley:14337851:media:anie202320183:anie202320183-math-0001" loading="lazy" class="section_image" src="/cms/asset/6d488cee-4bbc-467e-9857-bbd4ec683001/anie202320183-math-0001.png">
], and [2<img alt="urn:x-wiley:14337851:media:anie202320183:anie202320183-math-0002" loading="lazy" class="section_image" src="/cms/asset/831f6ae7-12c6-48a7-bb2a-fd633b8e4e87/anie202320183-math-0002.png">
0] planes of the Li<sub>2</sub>Sb. With the lithiation reaction going on, the Li<sub>2</sub>Sb phase finally evolves into the hexagonal Li<sub>3</sub>Sb phase (<i>P63/mmc</i>, No. 194). No polycrystalline or amorphous phases are observed with further lithiation, confirming that the single-crystalline Li<sub>3</sub>Sb is the final product.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202320183-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/9bcb9017-9421-42a2-8126-df9d4eb19364/anie202320183-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/9bcb9017-9421-42a2-8126-df9d4eb19364/anie202320183-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/2afc6386-a0a4-4e0b-baf8-f2be4ebe3137/anie202320183-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/9bcb9017-9421-42a2-8126-df9d4eb19364/anie202320183-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202320183-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202320183" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>(a–e) Time-lapse structural evolution taken at 0 s (a), 168 s (b), 264.5 s (c), 3600 s (d), and 4800 s (e), showing representative TEM images (top), FFT patterns (middle), and filtered HRTEM images (bottom) of the Sb nanoflake during the lithiation and delithiation process. (f) Enlarged image corresponding to the blue box in (e) during delithiation showing the dynamics of stacking faults. (g) The FFT pattern of the whole image of (e) shows a streaking line. (h) GPA analysis shows the emergence of stacking faults induced strain distribution. The low and high limits of the scalebar are −0.2 and 0.2. (i) Schematics for the structural evolution of Sb nanoflake during lithiation/delithiation process.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The electrochemical phase transformations during the delithiation process were also investigated because these transformations are critically related to the reversibility and stability of the Sb electrode. To minimize the effect of electron radiation, the electron beam was switched to the blank mode during the most delithiation period except for the time to capture images. The time-lapse HRTEM images and corresponding FFT patterns shown in Figure <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0003">3d</a>, <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0003">e</a> reveal that the lithiated mixture can fully transform back to the single-crystal Sb after the delithiation. Lithium extraction leads first to the formation of Li<sub>2</sub>Sb with hexagonal symmetry, followed by the formation of Sb. The enlarged HRTEM image indicates the highly ordered [10<img alt="urn:x-wiley:14337851:media:anie202320183:anie202320183-math-0003" loading="lazy" class="section_image" src="/cms/asset/8a59b22a-d877-45cd-9b1e-5a2b6baf372d/anie202320183-math-0003.png">
] planes of Sb with a lattice spacing of 0.30 nm after the delithiation for 4800 s.</p>
         
         <p>During the delithiation process, the Sb nanoflakes remain in good structural integrity. However, Figure <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0003">3f</a> shows that many defects are identified after delithiation (highlighted by dashed yellow rectangles) in the HRTEM images of delighted Sb nanoflakes. Close inspection of the Sb nanoflakes revealed the presence of a region with a high density of dislocations and stacking faults. The streaking between the Bragg spots in the corresponding FFT patterns (Figure <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0003">3g</a>) also indicates the formation of substantial planar structural defects, that is, stacking faults or nanodomains generated during delithiation. Besides, the strain distribution in the defect region of the delighted Sb nanoflakes was analyzed by GPA ϵ<sub>yy</sub> strain map (Figure <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0003">3h</a>), showing the stress concentration in that region. The Li<sup>+</sup> ions diffusion and defect motion lead to a significant stress accumulation, which may influence the Li<sup>+</sup> ions diffusion rate and the electrochemical reaction.<span><sup><a href="#anie202320183-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202320183-bib-0055_R_d97637201e1216" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Cell Rep. Phys. Sci.">27</a></sup></span> This results in an increased Li<sup>+</sup> ions concentration inhomogeneity and internal strain discontinuity, leading to severe degradation of the electrochemical performance (Figure <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0003">3i</a>).</p>
         
         <p>Based on the above in situ TEM results, the redox reactions involved during the first lithiation and delithiation are as follows:</p>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">Lithiation:
            <div class="inline-equation" id="anie202320183-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><img class="" src="/cms/asset/64a3fd53-006c-415c-9e93-a83b3a912d70/anie202320183-math-0004.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:14337851:media:anie202320183:anie202320183-math-0004" title="urn:x-wiley:14337851:media:anie202320183:anie202320183-math-0004" loading="lazy"></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>
            
            <div class="inline-equation" id="anie202320183-disp-0002"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><img class="" src="/cms/asset/46d8ec7e-e8e9-4e79-a639-82551d5154f0/anie202320183-math-0005.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:14337851:media:anie202320183:anie202320183-math-0005" title="urn:x-wiley:14337851:media:anie202320183:anie202320183-math-0005" loading="lazy"></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(2)</span></div>
            </div>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">Delithiation:
            <div class="inline-equation" id="anie202320183-disp-0003"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><img class="" src="/cms/asset/db5988b4-d68b-4294-b6e5-4ec1fcf95d8c/anie202320183-math-0006.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:14337851:media:anie202320183:anie202320183-math-0006" title="urn:x-wiley:14337851:media:anie202320183:anie202320183-math-0006" loading="lazy"></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(3)</span></div>
            
            <div class="inline-equation" id="anie202320183-disp-0004"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><img class="" src="/cms/asset/66b70a67-a632-4149-b554-bd591c216384/anie202320183-math-0007.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:14337851:media:anie202320183:anie202320183-math-0007" title="urn:x-wiley:14337851:media:anie202320183:anie202320183-math-0007" loading="lazy"></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(4)</span></div>
            </div>
         
         <p>As a parallel comparison, in situ sodiation observation on a single-crystalline Sb nanoflake was performed (Figure <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0004">4a</a>–<a href="#anie202320183-fig-0004">b</a>) using the same experimental setup but employing a sodium metal electrode with a naturally formed sodium oxide solid electrolyte. In this case, the pristine Sb nanoflakes and their FFT pattern showed a single-crystal structure with the zone axis along the [010] direction (Figure <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0004">4a</a>). As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0004">4a</a>, <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0004">b</a> (captured from Movie S2), HRTEM images were obtained at different stages of sodiation with the corresponding FFT patterns. Upon sodiation, the diffraction spots of Sb decrease progressively, while a blurry diffraction halo starts to appear (Figure <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0004">4a</a> panel 2 below) without observing any new peak in the diffraction pattern, indicating that part of the pristine Sb transforms into an amorphous phase termed Na<sub>x</sub>Sb. Then, more Na<sup>+</sup> ions react with the Sb nanoflakes to form a polycrystalline NaSb alloy with a monoclinic structure (<i>P21/c</i>, No. 14) while the amorphous phase maintains. Both the HRTEM image and the FFT pattern indicate the formation of the NaSb phase because the lattice fringe and diffraction spots match well with the [121] planes of the NaSb. With the sodiation reaction going on, the NaSb phase evolves into the hexagonal Na<sub>3</sub>Sb phase (<i>P63/mmc</i>, No. 194). The FFT pattern indicates that during most of the sodiation process, the sodiation product is a mixed phase of polycrystalline and amorphous phases. This is quite different from the lithiation process, during which the Sb nanoflake remains single-crystalline.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202320183-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/fa3c2de8-2f77-448d-9c20-a0288f145b93/anie202320183-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/fa3c2de8-2f77-448d-9c20-a0288f145b93/anie202320183-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/6186db97-fcee-4c52-b87c-132c48bb991f/anie202320183-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/fa3c2de8-2f77-448d-9c20-a0288f145b93/anie202320183-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202320183-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202320183" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>(a, b) Time-lapse TEM images taken at 0 s, 64.5 s, and 189 s, and corresponding filtered HRTEM images of the Sb nanoflake during the sodiation process. (c, d) Time-lapse TEM images taken at 220 s, 341 s, and 3600 s, and corresponding filtered HRTEM images during the desodiation process. (e) GPA analysis of the highlighted region in the image of 3600 s in (c). The inset shows the FFT pattern of the whole Sb nanoflake. (f) HAADF-STEM image shows the two interfaces after sodiation. (g–i) Enlarged image corresponding to the yellow dashed I and II box in (f) and corresponding lattice spacing maps and GPA patterns of the pristine and Na-intercalated phases. (j) Enlarged image and FFT pattern corresponding to the yellow dashed box III in (f). (k) Schematics for the structural evolution of Sb nanoflake during the sodiation/desodiation process.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Moreover, desodiation (Figure <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0004">4c</a>–<a href="#anie202320183-fig-0004">e</a>) is a significantly slower process than sodiation. The electron beam was also switched to the blank mode during the most desodiation period except for the time to capture images. As can be seen from the time-lapse HRTEM images and corresponding FFT patterns in Figure <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0003">3c</a>, the sodiated mixture can transform back to single-crystal Sb after desodiation. Na<sup>+</sup> ions extraction leads first to the formation of NaSb with monoclinic symmetry, followed by the formation of Sb with rhombohedral symmetry. It is worth noting that no streak line in the FFT pattern or localized strain distribution in the GPA map (Figure <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0004">4e</a>) is observed, suggesting a reaction mechanism significantly different from the lithiation process.</p>
         
         <p>Figure <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0004">4f</a> shows an atomic-scale HAADF image of sodiated Sb with two identified interfaces. High-resolution STEM images, corresponding lattice spacing maps, and GPA patterns of the pristine and Na-intercalated phases of Sb are displayed in Figure <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0004">4g</a>–<a href="#anie202320183-fig-0004">i</a>. The pristine Sb phase seen along [100] direction shows a periodicity of two-unit cells along [01<img alt="urn:x-wiley:14337851:media:anie202320183:anie202320183-math-0008" loading="lazy" class="section_image" src="/cms/asset/cc59e436-2b3e-489d-a897-cb154975a2f9/anie202320183-math-0008.png">
] direction (d1=2.7 Å and d2=2.0 Å). The intercalation phase Na<sub>x</sub>Sb domain is demonstrated (region II in Figure <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0004">4f</a>) and distinct from the pristine regions during sodiation. The lattice spacing maps and GPA patterns clearly show that the distance of Sb atoms along [01<img alt="urn:x-wiley:14337851:media:anie202320183:anie202320183-math-0009" loading="lazy" class="section_image" src="/cms/asset/9654a716-cd7f-4d2d-ad72-cd7e37216769/anie202320183-math-0009.png">
] direction varies upon Na<sup>+</sup> ions insertion (d=2.3 Å), demonstrating that the pristine Sb phase has been partly transformed into the intercalated phase due to the Na<sup>+</sup> ions intercalation. Another interface is located between the intercalation phase and the amorphous phase, confirming the generation of the amorphous phase during the sodiation reactions (Figure <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0004">4j</a>).</p>
         
         <p>Based on the above in situ TEM results, the reactions involved during the first sodiation and desodiation would be as follows.</p>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">Sodiation:
            <div class="inline-equation" id="anie202320183-disp-0005"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><img class="" src="/cms/asset/5272858a-b637-4330-8f3b-a66805820cd7/anie202320183-math-0010.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:14337851:media:anie202320183:anie202320183-math-0010" title="urn:x-wiley:14337851:media:anie202320183:anie202320183-math-0010" loading="lazy"></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(5)</span></div>
            
            <div class="inline-equation" id="anie202320183-disp-0006"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><img class="" src="/cms/asset/c89235e9-f1f4-4cee-88c4-ce951036dd2c/anie202320183-math-0011.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:14337851:media:anie202320183:anie202320183-math-0011" title="urn:x-wiley:14337851:media:anie202320183:anie202320183-math-0011" loading="lazy"></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(6)</span></div>
            
            <div class="inline-equation" id="anie202320183-disp-0007"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><img class="" src="/cms/asset/67de6b68-6eef-462a-a823-703555bfb8f9/anie202320183-math-0012.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:14337851:media:anie202320183:anie202320183-math-0012" title="urn:x-wiley:14337851:media:anie202320183:anie202320183-math-0012" loading="lazy"></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(7)</span></div>
            </div>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">Desodiation:
            <div class="inline-equation" id="anie202320183-disp-0008"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><img class="" src="/cms/asset/163f0285-91cb-486b-b344-86412b622d90/anie202320183-math-0013.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:14337851:media:anie202320183:anie202320183-math-0013" title="urn:x-wiley:14337851:media:anie202320183:anie202320183-math-0013" loading="lazy"></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(8)</span></div>
            
            <div class="inline-equation" id="anie202320183-disp-0009"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><img class="" src="/cms/asset/706a271c-5633-4abd-a20d-8ab513750c93/anie202320183-math-0014.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:14337851:media:anie202320183:anie202320183-math-0014" title="urn:x-wiley:14337851:media:anie202320183:anie202320183-math-0014" loading="lazy"></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(9)</span></div>
            
            <div class="inline-equation" id="anie202320183-disp-0010"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><img class="" src="/cms/asset/cfbfc306-c66c-4d39-993c-3904804a1812/anie202320183-math-0015.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:14337851:media:anie202320183:anie202320183-math-0015" title="urn:x-wiley:14337851:media:anie202320183:anie202320183-math-0015" loading="lazy"></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(10)</span></div>
            </div>
         
         <p>It is obvious that both lithiation and sodiation processes similarly undergo an alloying-dominant reaction pathway. However, the pristine Sb single crystals transform into amorphous Na<sub>x</sub>Sb and ultrafine metallic NaSb and Na<sub>3</sub>Sb nanoparticles (Figure <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0004">4k</a>), in contrast to the crystal-crystal transition during the Li<sup>+</sup> ions reaction. The formation of amorphous and polycrystalline phases plays an important role in cycling stability. First, the amorphous phase having a long-range disordered structure averages the stress distribution in different directions, because bonds can be formed randomly in the amorphous phase and do not have to be along certain orientations as they do in the crystalline phases.<span><sup><a href="#anie202320183-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202320183-bib-0056_R_d97637201e1424" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Small">28</a></sup></span> Thus, reducing the stress anisotropy and improving the structural stability.<span><sup><a href="#anie202320183-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202320183-bib-0057_R_d97637201e1428" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29">29</a></sup></span> Second, amorphous phase can avoid the formation of large cracks/isolation of active materials, which is beneficial for preserving the connectivity and charge transport within the material<span><sup><a href="#anie202320183-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202320183-bib-0060_R_d97637201e1431" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30">30</a></sup></span> The Na<sub>x</sub>Sb phase can accommodate more strain than ordered crystalline Sb during Li<sup>+</sup> ions insertion and extraction. Third, the formation of polycrystalline NaSb and Na<sub>3</sub>Sb can also release a large amount of stress due to the large amount of grain boundaries and relatively free movement of the grains, favoring maintaining good structural integrity. Therefore, many efforts are devoted to synthesizing amorphous electrode materials.<span><sup><a href="#anie202320183-bib-0063" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202320183-bib-0063_R_d97637201e1440" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31">31</a></sup></span> In our case, amorphous Na<sub>x</sub>Sb phases are in situ generated without sophisticated synthesis and expensive reagents.</p>
         
         <p>To confirm the phase transformations identified by in situ HRTEM and FFT analyses, in situ SAED (Movie S3 and S4) was also performed to further monitor real-time phase evolution during the lithiation and sodiation processes (Figure <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0005">5</a>). In situ SAED patterns are sequentially captured from a large area of a few micrometers to collect structural information contributed from more material sampling, offering phase identification with higher precision and better statistical validation. Figure <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0005">5a</a>, <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0005">e</a> shows the SAED intensity profile as a function of reaction time during in situ lithiation and sodiation of Sb, which is derived from radially integrated SAED from the regions highlighted by dashed yellow sectors and represented by the false colors. Before lithiation/sodiation, characteristic diffraction rings for Sb corresponding to [101] or [012] peaks are observed (Figure S6). As shown in Figure S6a, the diffraction peaks of pristine Sb immediately shift left as soon as Li<sup>+</sup> ions are inserted, indicating that the spacing of Sb increased after Li<sup>+</sup> ions intercalation. With more alkali metal ion insertion, several new diffraction rings appear, initially faint but becoming stronger over time (Figure S6b). During the sodiation process, the lattice expansion and the appearance of new diffraction points corresponding to NaSb and Na<sub>3</sub>Sb were observed, as well as the presence of an amorphous Na<sub>x</sub>Sb phase (Figure S6c, d). Therefore, multistep phase transitions from Sb<img alt="urn:x-wiley:14337851:media:anie202320183:anie202320183-math-0016" loading="lazy" class="section_image" src="/cms/asset/5092430f-8922-4139-bc55-fb12f3e391b8/anie202320183-math-0016.png">
Li<sub>2</sub>Sb<img alt="urn:x-wiley:14337851:media:anie202320183:anie202320183-math-0017" loading="lazy" class="section_image" src="/cms/asset/981e1916-97b0-4b73-997c-9ef75b32d31f/anie202320183-math-0017.png">
Li<sub>3</sub>Sb during lithiation and Sb<img alt="urn:x-wiley:14337851:media:anie202320183:anie202320183-math-0018" loading="lazy" class="section_image" src="/cms/asset/b3a1e832-38eb-4b1f-93ee-16c8b534b986/anie202320183-math-0018.png">
Na<sub>x</sub>Sb<img alt="urn:x-wiley:14337851:media:anie202320183:anie202320183-math-0019" loading="lazy" class="section_image" src="/cms/asset/645a4f24-e5b9-4dc8-8e61-d0b404e7f39b/anie202320183-math-0019.png">
NaSb<img alt="urn:x-wiley:14337851:media:anie202320183:anie202320183-math-0020" loading="lazy" class="section_image" src="/cms/asset/ee3784c4-098a-404c-88b2-bca1fd401751/anie202320183-math-0020.png">
Na<sub>3</sub>Sb during sodiation are identified. Additionally, representative SAED patterns captured at each stage confirm that the pristine Sb remains single-crystalline during lithiation, while the single-crystalline Sb transforms to a mix of polycrystalline and amorphous states during sodiation. These observations are well consistent with the HRTEM and FFT analyses presented above.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202320183-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/98d1a422-07de-4bd4-b691-f8c3df275b1f/anie202320183-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/98d1a422-07de-4bd4-b691-f8c3df275b1f/anie202320183-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/5b6c2b7b-bb5f-4bb0-9886-c6f9c450dcc8/anie202320183-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/98d1a422-07de-4bd4-b691-f8c3df275b1f/anie202320183-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202320183-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202320183" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>(a, e) SAED intensity profile as a function of lithiation/sodiation time and representative SAED patterns captured in real-time. In situ XRD contour maps and the corresponding strains during charge/discharge processes of the Sb electrode as (b, c) LIB's anode and (f, g) SIB's anode. In situ Raman spectra in contour maps acquired along charge/discharge processes as (d) LIB's anode (h) and SIB's anode. The different two-stage reaction mechanisms of the Sb electrode in (i) LIB and (j) SIB.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Combining both in situ SAED and in situ HRTEM results, we further confirm the identical phase transformations and microstructure evolutions by both in situ XRD and in situ Raman techniques during the lithiation/delithiation and sodiation/desodiation reactions. In situ XRD patterns of Sb electrodes for both LIBs and SIBs show the typical crystal feature before cycling, as shown in Figure <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0005">5b</a>, <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0005">f</a>, marked in red arrows in three separated angle sections (21.5–30°, 37.5–42.5°, 58.5–70.5°), but the diffraction peaks are different for LIBs and SIBs, and more data, including in situ XRD patterns in waterfalls and patterns in 3D styles with vivid information on peak position and intensity, are shown in Figure S7a, b and Figure S8a, b. Specifically, the diffraction peaks of Sb in LIB show the typical two stages of phase changes (Sb<img alt="urn:x-wiley:14337851:media:anie202320183:anie202320183-math-0021" loading="lazy" class="section_image" src="/cms/asset/43b1940e-ebd2-45a1-b429-b59319d198c6/anie202320183-math-0021.png">
Li<sub>2</sub>Sb<img alt="urn:x-wiley:14337851:media:anie202320183:anie202320183-math-0022" loading="lazy" class="section_image" src="/cms/asset/2fce2572-7378-4e57-adb6-e655f34c4c88/anie202320183-math-0022.png">
Li<sub>3</sub>Sb) during discharge/charge processes. In contrast, the diffraction peaks of Sb in SIBs are obviously different from the situations in LIBs, displaying the polycrystalline and an apparent amorphous state of Na<sub>x</sub>Sb alloys referring to those wide peaks in those three regions. Despite having slightly different peak intensities, both can return to their original phases after the in situ discharge/charge measurements. The corresponding strains in the Sb electrodes for LIBs and SIBs are calculated via Gauss analysis and shown in Figure <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0005">5c</a>, <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0005">g</a>. The strains are gradually growing to their maximum values at the end of the discharge process, relating to the lithiation and sodiation with large volume expansion. While the strain-releasing rate during desodiation is lower than that of delithiation during charging, which is highly ascribed to the formation of polycrystalline and amorphous states during sodiation. Therefore, the Sb electrode suffers fewer inner stains as the SIBs anode to the LIBs anode due to the different redox reaction mechanisms.</p>
         
         <p>The polycrystalline and amorphous states of the Sb electrode as the SIBs anode are more apparent in in situ Raman patterns seen in Figure <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0005">5d</a>, <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0005">h</a>. The two Raman shift peaks of the Sb electrode at 115 and 150 cm<sup>−1</sup> are fully converted to Li<sub>2</sub>Sb and then Li<sub>3</sub>Sb at the end of discharge in LIBs, while they are turned to polycrystalline and amorphous states in SIBs by forming Na<sub>x</sub>Sb, further demonstrating this phenomenon, and both are recovered to pure Sb phases at the end of charge states. More in situ Raman data, including Raman shifts in waterfall and patterns in 3D styles with vivid information of peak shift and intensity, are shown in Figure S7c–d and Figure S8c–d, exhibiting the reversibility of Sb electrode, different two-stage alloying/de-alloying reaction mechanisms, with/without polycrystalline and amorphous intermediates. Therefore, the full redox reactions of Sb in LIBs follow the two-stage alloying/de-alloying mechanism (Sb<img alt="urn:x-wiley:14337851:media:anie202320183:anie202320183-math-0023" loading="lazy" class="section_image" src="/cms/asset/6d83287e-8095-4a39-8d3e-fdb3f0eaa551/anie202320183-math-0023.png">
Li<sub>2</sub>Sb<img alt="urn:x-wiley:14337851:media:anie202320183:anie202320183-math-0024" loading="lazy" class="section_image" src="/cms/asset/0b7f75d0-c9e3-42e5-997c-16a7d0c04bf3/anie202320183-math-0024.png">
Li<sub>3</sub>Sb) in Figure <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0005">5i</a>, while the redox reactions in SIBs display a special two-stage alloying/de-alloying mechanism with polycrystalline phases and amorphous states as the intermediates, as shown in Figure <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0005">5j</a>.</p>
         
         <p>In situ low-magnification TEM examinations were also carried out to explore the morphological dynamics of Sb nanoflakes during electrochemical reactions with Li and Na cations. The time-sequential TEM images of dynamic morphology evolutions during in situ lithiation and sodiation are displayed in Figure S9, which are representatively selected from original real-time movies (Movies S5 and S6). After applying a constant negative bias of −2.0 V to the counter electrode, the controllable electrochemical reaction of Sb nanoflakes would be triggered immediately. Figure S9a, c show the propagation of electrochemical reaction fronts during the lithiation and sodiation processes. Notably, the surface of Sb nanoflakes developed several irregular corners during the lithiation process (Figure S9b), and these corners′ strain-induced TEM diffraction contrasts the crystal nature of the reaction products, which is consistent with the HRTEM results. However, in Figure S9d, the Sb crystals transformed to spherical or ellipsoidal shapes during the sodiation, suggesting that the corners of the Sb react more readily to ‘‘round-off’’ sharp corners as the sodiation occurs. This is in contrast to the lithiation case, in which sharp corners are generated during the reaction. Furthermore, the uniform TEM contrast of the reaction products implies the generation of the amorphous phase during sodiation. From the standpoint of mechanical integrity, the geometry of the reaction front also has a direct impact on the fracture of the Sb nanoflake. The sharp corners and the associated stress concentrations may lead to fracture at those sharp corners, whereas the rounded border of the Na<sub>x</sub>Sb during sodiation enhances its tolerance to fracture and alleviates potential mechanical failure during prolonged cycling. Furthermore, according to the statistical results of the cross-section expansion and length elongation (Figure S9e–g), the cross-section expansion and length elongation during sodiation is slightly larger than that during lithiation. Figure S9h–j further shows the cross-sectional area changes for the specific region of Sb nanoflakes during lithiation and sodiation. Evidently, the areal expansion of the Sb anode after sodiation (242 %) for 35 s is 1.5 times larger than during lithiation (165 %). The measured reaction rate during sodiation is slightly slower than that of Li<sup>+</sup> ions diffusion, due to the larger radius and heavier mass of Na<sup>+</sup> ions relative to that of Li<sup>+</sup> ions.</p>
         
         <p>It is a common belief that with larger volume expansion, the resultant sodiation stress will be far greater than its lithiation counterpart, leading to deteriorating cycling stability. Surprisingly, the electrochemical performances of Sb anodes in LIBs and SIBs revealed the opposite phenomenon. To elucidate such an intriguing observation, FEA was carried out using the same dimension as the Sb nanoflakes observed by the TEM.<span><sup><a href="#anie202320183-bib-0068" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202320183-bib-0068_R_d97637201e1582" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Matter">32</a></sup></span> It should be noted that Sb undergoes several consecutive phase transformations when alloyed with Li and Na elements. Herein, the mechanical properties of stable Li−Sb and Na−Sb alloys are first calculated by DFT. The bulk modulus, shear modulus, and Young's modulus were found approximately following a linear correlation with the M (M=Li, Na) content in the alloy (Figure <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0006">6a</a>–<a href="#anie202320183-fig-0006">c</a>). Such a linear correlation allows for the FEA based on a continuous model where the modulus decreases linearly with increasing M concentration in the alloy. Two types of stress are vital to the evaluation of the failure of the Sb electrode, namely the surface stress and the interior stress in the vicinity of the reaction front. In general, the surface residual stress upon cation insertion is the key to crack formation. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0006">6d</a>, <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0006">e</a>, despite a similar stress distribution, the surface stress concentrated on the edge of the Sb particle is greater for lithiation compared to sodiation, indicating potential crack formation initiating from the particle's edge and worse mechanical stability during lithiation of the Sb particle. Such a counterintuitive result is arising from the much lower Young's and bulk modulus of Na<sub>x</sub>Sb compared to Li<sub>x</sub>Sb, which effectively alleviates the diffusion-induced stress despite a larger volume expansion of sodiation. On the other hand, the interior stress induced by the progression of the reaction front facilitates the diffusion of alkali-ions, which in turn promotes the Li cation's insertion, resulting in a better rate performance of the batteries. However, a too-large interior stress concentrated near the particle surface also causes crack formation, and consequently results in electrode failure. At the same stage (Figures <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0006">6f</a>, <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0006">g</a>), the interior stress of the sodiation is much smaller than that of lithiation (Figures <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0006">6h</a>, <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0006">i</a>). Such a smaller stress accounts for the better stability of the SIBs, leading to the sluggish rate capability compared to the LIBs as shown in Figure <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0002">2d</a>.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202320183-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ca5e1eee-f9f9-44bc-8354-7f626ba087a8/anie202320183-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/ca5e1eee-f9f9-44bc-8354-7f626ba087a8/anie202320183-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/1ad6f79c-c9ef-448a-bd88-a021ed1e1f75/anie202320183-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ca5e1eee-f9f9-44bc-8354-7f626ba087a8/anie202320183-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202320183-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202320183" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Calculated (a) bulk, (b) shear and (c) Young's modulus of Sb−M alloy at different M concentrations. First principal stress distribution on the surface of the Sb particle during (d) lithiation and (e) sodiation. The stress is normalized to the theoretical Young's modulus of Sb; Contour plots showing normalized (f) Li and (g) Na concentration and the corresponding (h) lithiation- and (i) sodiation-induced stress upon their reaction with Sb particles. (j) Volume changes of Li<sub>3</sub>Sb and Na<sub>3</sub>Sb to Sb. Projection of the charge density difference of (k) Li<sub>3</sub>Sb and (l) Na<sub>3</sub>Sb.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Subsequently, the intrinsic factors that determine the volume expansion of Na<sub>3</sub>Sb and Li<sub>3</sub>Sb from the atomic scale were revealed, as well as the possible correlation with cycling stability. DFT calculations show that the average atomic and unit cell volumes of Na<sub>3</sub>Sb are significantly larger than those of Li<sub>3</sub>Sb (Figure <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0006">6j</a>), which is well consistent with experimental observations and FEA, and it should be associated with the strength of the interactions between heterogeneous atoms. Figure <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0006">6k</a> presents the projection of the charge density difference of Li<sub>3</sub>Sb on the [110] plane. It can be seen that after the formation of Li<sub>3</sub>Sb crystals, there is an enrichment of transfer charges between Sb and Li atoms, showing characteristics of covalent-like bonds. In addition, the transferred charges tend to be distributed around the Sb atoms, and the characteristics of an ionic-like bond can also be detected. In contrast, Na and Sb atoms in Na<sub>3</sub>Sb crystals also form a similar charge distribution to that in Li<sub>3</sub>Sb, but the total amount of charge transfer is less (Figure <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0006">6l</a>). Since Li and Na atoms have similar electronic shell structures, we believe that the interactions between atoms in Na<sub>3</sub>Sb crystals are significantly weaker than those of Li<sub>3</sub>Sb, leading to a larger average atomic volume of Na<sub>3</sub>Sb than Li<sub>3</sub>Sb.<span><sup><a href="#anie202320183-bib-0069" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202320183-bib-0069_R_d97637201e1687" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33">33</a></sup></span> Although the average atomic volume of Na<sub>3</sub>Sb is larger, the weak atomic interaction contact reduces the energy required during the insertion/extraction of Na ions. Hence, the structure can maintain better stability, thereby improving the cycle performance of the electrode.</p>
         
         <p>To better understand the thermodynamics of the electrochemical reactions of Sb with Li and Na and their corresponding reaction kinetics, first-principles DFT calculations and kinetic Mento Carlo simulations were conducted.<span><sup><a href="#anie202320183-bib-0072" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202320183-bib-0072_R_d97637201e1696" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34">34</a></sup></span> Ground state phase diagrams of the Li−Sb and Na−Sb are constructed using the DFT energies of known compounds in the Open Quantum Materials Database.<span><sup><a href="#anie202320183-bib-0075" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202320183-bib-0075_R_d97637201e1699" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - npj Comput. Mater.">35</a></sup></span> According to the DFT calculations, Li<sub>2</sub>Sb and Li<sub>3</sub>Sb are the ground-state phases in the Li−Sb binary systems (Figure <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0007">7a</a>), confirming the experimental observations. The calculated voltage profile also shows reasonable agreement with the experimentally measured discharge voltage curve, as shown in Figure <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0007">7a</a>. While in Na−Sb systems, two ground phases (NaSb and Na<sub>3</sub>Sb) are identified experimentally with corresponding voltage profiles slightly higher than the discharge voltage curve.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202320183-fig-0007"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/7b24cb5a-de60-467b-9de5-bb8349351d35/anie202320183-fig-0007-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/7b24cb5a-de60-467b-9de5-bb8349351d35/anie202320183-fig-0007-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ea6ee964-dc20-4840-a594-f7e8386715c2/anie202320183-fig-0007-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/7b24cb5a-de60-467b-9de5-bb8349351d35/anie202320183-fig-0007-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 7<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202320183-fig-0007&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202320183" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Alkalis (Li, Na) and Sb electrochemical reaction thermodynamics and kinetics. (a) Li−Sb convex hull and reaction voltage profile. (b) Na−Sb convex hull and reaction voltage profile. (c) Calculated room temperature ion diffusivities in Li(Na)-Sb reaction products. Ions diffusion networks with dominant migration pathways and corresponding barriers in (d) Li<sub>2</sub>Sb, (e) Li<sub>3</sub>Sb, (f) NaSb, (g) Na<sub>3</sub>Sb.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>However, the amorphization of the Na<sub>x</sub>Sb phase increases its energy and decreases the resulting voltage plateau, as shown in Figure <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0007">7b</a>, leading to a better agreement with the discharge voltage. While Li<sup>+</sup> ions diffusions were faster with activation energies as low as 0.27 eV and 0.33 eV for Li<sub>2</sub>Sb and Li<sub>3</sub>Sb (Figure <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0007">7c</a>–<a href="#anie202320183-fig-0007">e</a>), confirming the as-measured superior rate capacity in LIBs. In the contrast, Na<sup>+</sup> ions migrations in crystalline NaSb and Na<sub>3</sub>Sb are even more facile, with activation energies as low as 0.14 eV and 0.15 eV (Figure <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0007">7c</a>, <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0007">f</a>, <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0007">g</a>). However, the as-observed polycrystalline and amorphous Na<sub>x</sub>Sb phases could block the Na<sup>+</sup> ions diffusion channels in these phases, leading to a severely impaired rate capacity of Sb as a SIB electrode, due to the absence of crystallinity of the particle, which is consistent with the rate performance as shown in Figure <a href="#anie202320183-fig-0002">2d</a>.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202320183-sec-0003">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202320183-sec-0003-title">3 Conclusions</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, this study has demonstrated the systematically comparative study of electrochemical performance and reaction mechanisms of Sb anode during reaction with Li<sup>+</sup> and Na<sup>+</sup> ions using a combination of in situ TEM coupled with GPA, in situ XRD, and in situ Raman techniques complemented by theoretical simulations and electrochemical methods. Although Na<sup>+</sup> ions have larger ionic radii than Li<sup>+</sup> ions, causing larger volumetric changes during (de)sodiation cycling, it has been observed that the Sb anode in SIBs shows better both cycling and structural stability. With the support of multiple in situ experimental methods combined with FEA simulations, we reveal that Sb reacted with Li<sup>+</sup> ions undergoes a stepwise crystal transition alloying/dealloying process and is accompanied by large lattice strain which consequently led to structural failure and fast capacity decay. The reaction of Sb with Na<sup>+</sup> ions, however, appears to be more complex and shows a special two-stage alloying/de-alloying mechanism with polycrystalline phases and amorphous states as intermediates featuring enhanced resilience to mechanical damage, which contributes to superior stable cycling stability. Besides, as unveiled by the complementary DFT and FEA simulations, it demonstrates that the better mechanical properties and weaker atomic interaction of Na−Sb alloys than Li−Sb alloys favor mitigating mechanical fracture, accounting for enhanced structural stability. Our findings in this work provide some insight into the mechanistic understanding of alkali-ion storage mechanisms in alloying-type anodes, and offer important implications for performance optimization and rational design of high-capacity and durable anodes used beyond Li-based battery systems.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
